<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Printing</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Process for reproducing text and images using a master form or template</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the process of reproducing text and images. For the handwriting method, see <a href="/wiki/Block_letters" title="Block letters">block letters</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Print_(disambiguation)" title="Print (disambiguation)">Print (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_printing" title="Template:History of printing"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_printing" title="Template talk:History of printing"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_printing&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background: #eef; text-align: center;">Research</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Marketing" title="Template:Marketing"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Marketing" title="Template talk:Marketing"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Marketing&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Printing</b> is a process for mass reproducing text and <a href="/wiki/Printmaking" title="Printmaking">images</a> using a master form or template. The earliest non-paper products involving printing include <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seal" title="Cylinder seal">cylinder seals</a> and objects such as the <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_Cylinder" title="Cyrus Cylinder">Cyrus Cylinder</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cylinders_of_Nabonidus" title="Cylinders of Nabonidus">Cylinders of Nabonidus</a>. The earliest known form of printing as applied to paper was <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock printing</a>, which appeared in China before 220 AD for cloth printing. However, it would not be applied to paper until the seventh century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cave_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-cave-1">[1]</a></sup> Later developments in printing technology include the <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> invented by <a href="/wiki/Bi_Sheng" title="Bi Sheng">Bi Sheng</a> around 1040 AD<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Great_Chinese_Inventions_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Great_Chinese_Inventions-2">[2]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> invented by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Johannes Gutenberg</a> in the 15th century. The technology of printing played a key role in the development of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">Scientific Revolution</a> and laid the material basis for the modern knowledge-based economy and the spread of learning to the masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_printing" title="History of printing">History of printing</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Woodblock_printing">Woodblock printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Woodblock printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">Woodblock printing</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">Woodblock printing</a> is a technique for printing text, images or patterns that was used widely throughout East Asia. It originated in China in antiquity as a method of printing on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Textiles" title="Textiles">textiles</a> and later on paper. As a method of printing on cloth, the earliest surviving examples from China date to before 220 A.D.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_East_Asia">In East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: In East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jingangjing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3438" data-file-width="4917" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/220px-Jingangjing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/330px-Jingangjing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/440px-Jingangjing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jingangjing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The intricate frontispiece of the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Sutra" title="Diamond Sutra">Diamond Sutra</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang-dynasty</a> China, 868 AD (<a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_printing_in_East_Asia" title="History of printing in East Asia">History of printing in East Asia</a></div>
<p>The earliest surviving woodblock printed fragments are from China. They are of silk printed with flowers in three colours from the Han Dynasty (before 220 A.D.). The earliest examples of woodblock printing on paper appear in the mid-seventh century in China.
</p><p>By the ninth century, printing on paper had taken off, and the first extant complete printed book containing its date is the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Sutra" title="Diamond Sutra">Diamond Sutra</a> (<a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>) of 868.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>  By the tenth century, 400,000 copies of some sutras and pictures were printed, and the Confucian classics were in print. A skilled printer could print up to 2,000 double-page sheets per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Needham_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Needham-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Printing spread early to <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and Japan, which also used Chinese <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logograms</a>, but the technique was also used in <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turpan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> using a number of other scripts. This technique then spread to Persia and Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carter_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-6">[6]</a></sup> This technique was transmitted to Europe by around 1400 and was used on paper for <a href="/wiki/Old_master_print" title="Old master print">old master prints</a> and <a href="/wiki/Playing_card" title="Playing card">playing cards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Middle_East">In Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: In Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Block printing, called <i><a href="/wiki/Tarsh" title="Tarsh">tarsh</a></i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, developed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Arab_Egypt" title="History of Arab Egypt">Arabic Egypt</a> during the ninth and tenth centuries, mostly for prayers and <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">amulets</a>. There is some evidence to suggest that these print blocks made from non-wood materials, possibly <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, lead, or clay. The techniques employed are uncertain. Block printing later went out of use in Islamic <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Renaissance" title="Timurid Renaissance">Timurid Renaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>  The printing technique in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> was embraced reproducing texts on paper strips and supplying them in different copies to meet the demand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloom8_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloom8-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Europe">In Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: In Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Christopher_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1454" data-file-width="1067" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Saint_Christopher_001.jpg/200px-Saint_Christopher_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Saint_Christopher_001.jpg/300px-Saint_Christopher_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Saint_Christopher_001.jpg/400px-Saint_Christopher_001.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Christopher_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The earliest known <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcut</a>, 1423, <a href="/wiki/Buxheim" title="Buxheim">Buxheim</a>, with hand-colouring</div></div></div>
<p>Block printing first came to Europe as a method for printing on cloth, where it was common by 1300. Images printed on cloth for religious purposes could be quite large and elaborate. When paper became relatively easily available, around 1400, the technique transferred very quickly to small <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcut</a> religious images and <a href="/wiki/Playing_card" title="Playing card">playing cards</a> printed on paper. These <a href="/wiki/Old_master_print" title="Old master print">prints</a> produced in very large numbers from about 1425 onward.
</p><p>Around the mid-fifteenth-century, <i>block-books</i>, woodcut books with both text and images, usually carved in the same block, emerged as a cheaper alternative to manuscripts and books printed with <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a>. These were all short heavily illustrated works, the bestsellers of the day, repeated in many different block-book versions: the <a href="/wiki/Ars_moriendi" title="Ars moriendi">Ars moriendi</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Biblia_pauperum" title="Biblia pauperum">Biblia pauperum</a> were the most common.  There is still some controversy among scholars as to whether their introduction preceded or, the majority view, followed the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a>, with the range of estimated dates being between about 1440 and 1460.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Movable-type_printing">Movable-type printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Movable-type printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E4%BA%94%E8%B4%AF%E5%AE%9D%E5%8D%B7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="589" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/%E4%BA%94%E8%B4%AF%E5%AE%9D%E5%8D%B7.jpg/200px-%E4%BA%94%E8%B4%AF%E5%AE%9D%E5%8D%B7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/%E4%BA%94%E8%B4%AF%E5%AE%9D%E5%8D%B7.jpg/300px-%E4%BA%94%E8%B4%AF%E5%AE%9D%E5%8D%B7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/%E4%BA%94%E8%B4%AF%E5%AE%9D%E5%8D%B7.jpg/400px-%E4%BA%94%E8%B4%AF%E5%AE%9D%E5%8D%B7.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E4%BA%94%E8%B4%AF%E5%AE%9D%E5%8D%B7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copperplate of 1215–1216 5000 <a href="/wiki/Cash_(currency)" title="Cash (currency)">cash</a> paper money with ten bronze movable types</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/220px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/330px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/440px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jikji" title="Jikji">Jikji</a>, "Selected Teachings of Buddhist Sages and Son Masters" from Korea, the earliest known book printed with movable metal type, 1377. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_Nationale_de_France" title="Bibliothèque Nationale de France">Bibliothèque Nationale de France</a>, Paris</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">Movable type</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Western_typography" title="History of Western typography">History of Western typography</a></div>
<p><b>Movable type</b> is the system of printing and <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">typography</a> using movable pieces of metal type, made by casting from <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(printing)" title="Matrix (printing)">matrices</a> struck by <a href="/wiki/Punchcutting" title="Punchcutting">letterpunches</a>. <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">Movable type</a> allowed for much more flexible processes than hand copying or block printing.
</p><p>Around 1040, the first known movable type system was created in China by <a href="/wiki/Bi_Sheng" title="Bi Sheng">Bi Sheng</a> out of <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Great_Chinese_Inventions_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Great_Chinese_Inventions-2">[2]</a></sup> Bi Sheng used clay type, which broke easily, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wang_Zhen_(official)" title="Wang Zhen (official)">Wang Zhen</a> by 1298 had carved a more durable type from wood. He also developed a complex system of revolving tables and number-association with written Chinese characters that made typesetting and printing more efficient. Still, the main method in use there remained woodblock printing (xylography), which "proved to be cheaper and more efficient for printing Chinese, with its thousands of characters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper movable type printing originated in China at the beginning of the 12th century. It was used in large-scale printing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_money" title="Paper money">paper money</a>
issued by the Northern Song dynasty. Movable type spread to Korea during the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> dynasty.
</p><p>Around 1230, Koreans invented a metal type movable printing using bronze. The <a href="/wiki/Jikji" title="Jikji">Jikji</a>, published in 1377, is the earliest known metal printed book. Type-casting was used, adapted from the method of casting coins. The character was cut in beech wood, which was then pressed into a soft clay to form a mould, and bronze poured into the mould, and finally the type was polished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Eastern metal movable type was spread to Europe between the late 14th and early 15th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The Korean form of metal movable type was described by the French scholar Henri-Jean Martin as "extremely similar to Gutenberg's".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>  Authoritative historians <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frances_Gies_and_Joseph_Gies" title="Frances Gies and Joseph Gies">Frances Gies and Joseph Gies</a> claimed that "The Asian priority of invention movable type is now firmly established, and that Chinese-Korean technique, or a report of it traveled westward is almost certain."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frances&amp;Joseph_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frances&amp;Joseph-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metal_movable_type.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1520" data-file-width="2288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Metal_movable_type.jpg/220px-Metal_movable_type.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Metal_movable_type.jpg/330px-Metal_movable_type.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Metal_movable_type.jpg/440px-Metal_movable_type.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metal_movable_type.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A case of cast metal type pieces and typeset matter in a composing stick</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_printing_press">The printing press</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The printing press">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">Printing press</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:De_uitvinding_van_de_boekdrukkunst,_anoniem,_Museum_Plantin-Moretus,_PK_OPB_0186_005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5100" data-file-width="7016" decoding="async" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/De_uitvinding_van_de_boekdrukkunst%2C_anoniem%2C_Museum_Plantin-Moretus%2C_PK_OPB_0186_005.jpg/450px-De_uitvinding_van_de_boekdrukkunst%2C_anoniem%2C_Museum_Plantin-Moretus%2C_PK_OPB_0186_005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/De_uitvinding_van_de_boekdrukkunst%2C_anoniem%2C_Museum_Plantin-Moretus%2C_PK_OPB_0186_005.jpg/675px-De_uitvinding_van_de_boekdrukkunst%2C_anoniem%2C_Museum_Plantin-Moretus%2C_PK_OPB_0186_005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/De_uitvinding_van_de_boekdrukkunst%2C_anoniem%2C_Museum_Plantin-Moretus%2C_PK_OPB_0186_005.jpg/900px-De_uitvinding_van_de_boekdrukkunst%2C_anoniem%2C_Museum_Plantin-Moretus%2C_PK_OPB_0186_005.jpg 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:De_uitvinding_van_de_boekdrukkunst,_anoniem,_Museum_Plantin-Moretus,_PK_OPB_0186_005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The invention of printing, anonymous, design by <a href="/wiki/Stradanus" title="Stradanus">Stradanus</a>, collection <a href="/wiki/Plantin-Moretus_Museum" title="Plantin-Moretus Museum">Plantin-Moretus Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Around 1450, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Johannes Gutenberg</a> introduced the first movable type printing system in Europe. He advanced innovations in casting type based on a matrix and <a href="/wiki/Hand_mould" title="Hand mould">hand mould</a>, adaptations to the screw-press, the use of an oil-based ink, and the creation of a softer and more absorbent paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Gutenberg was the first to create his type pieces from an alloy of lead, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, copper and bismuth – the same components still used today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Johannes Gutenberg started work on his <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> around 1436, in partnership with Andreas Dritzehen – whom he had previously instructed in gem-cutting – and Andreas Heilmann, the owner of a paper mill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meggs58-69_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-meggs58-69-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2022)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Compared to <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock printing</a>, movable type page setting and printing using a press was faster and more durable. Also, the metal type pieces were sturdier and the lettering more uniform, leading to <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">typography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Font" title="Font">fonts</a>. The high quality and relatively low price of the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a> (1455) established the superiority of movable type for Western languages. The printing press rapidly spread across Europe, leading up to the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, and later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spread_of_the_printing_press" title="Spread of the printing press">all around the world</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mikl%C3%B3s_Andor_in_the_page-setting_room_of_Athenaeum_Printing_House_-_cca._1920_(1).tiff"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2771" data-file-width="4056" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Mikl%C3%B3s_Andor_in_the_page-setting_room_of_Athenaeum_Printing_House_-_cca._1920_%281%29.tiff/lossy-page1-220px-Mikl%C3%B3s_Andor_in_the_page-setting_room_of_Athenaeum_Printing_House_-_cca._1920_%281%29.tiff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Mikl%C3%B3s_Andor_in_the_page-setting_room_of_Athenaeum_Printing_House_-_cca._1920_%281%29.tiff/lossy-page1-330px-Mikl%C3%B3s_Andor_in_the_page-setting_room_of_Athenaeum_Printing_House_-_cca._1920_%281%29.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Mikl%C3%B3s_Andor_in_the_page-setting_room_of_Athenaeum_Printing_House_-_cca._1920_%281%29.tiff/lossy-page1-440px-Mikl%C3%B3s_Andor_in_the_page-setting_room_of_Athenaeum_Printing_House_-_cca._1920_%281%29.tiff.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mikl%C3%B3s_Andor_in_the_page-setting_room_of_Athenaeum_Printing_House_-_cca._1920_(1).tiff" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page-setting room – c. 1920</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Time_Life" title="Time Life">Time Life</a> magazine called Gutenberg's innovations in movable type printing the most important invention of the second millennium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotary_printing_press">Rotary printing press</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rotary printing press">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rotary_printing_press" title="Rotary printing press">Rotary printing press</a></div>
<p>The <b>rotary printing press</b> was invented by <a href="/wiki/Richard_March_Hoe" title="Richard March Hoe">Richard March Hoe</a> in 1843. It uses impressions curved around a cylinder to print on long continuous rolls of paper or other substrates. Rotary drum printing was later significantly improved by <a href="/wiki/William_Bullock_(inventor)" title="William Bullock (inventor)">William Bullock</a>. There are multiple types of rotary printing press technologies that are still used today: sheetfed <a href="/wiki/Offset_printing" title="Offset printing">offset</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotogravure" title="Rotogravure">rotogravure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flexography" title="Flexography">flexographic</a> printing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_printing_technology">Conventional printing technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Conventional printing technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All printing process are concerned with two kinds of areas on the final output:
</p>
<ol><li>Image area (printing areas)</li>
<li>Non-image area (non-printing areas)</li></ol>
<p>After the information has been prepared for production (the <a href="/wiki/Prepress" title="Prepress">prepress</a> step), each printing process has definitive means of separating the image from the non-image areas.
</p><p>Conventional printing has four types of process:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Planographic_printing" title="Planographic printing">Planographics</a>, in which the printing and non-printing areas are on the same plane surface and the difference between them is maintained chemically or by physical properties, the examples are: <a href="/wiki/Offset_printing" title="Offset printing">offset</a> <a href="/wiki/Lithography" title="Lithography">lithography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collotype" title="Collotype">collotype</a>, and screenless printing.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Relief_printing" title="Relief printing">Relief</a>, in which the printing areas are on a plane surface and the non printing areas are below the surface, examples: flexography and letterpress.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Intaglio_(printmaking)" title="Intaglio (printmaking)">Intaglio</a>, in which the non-printing areas are on a plane surface and the printing area are etched or engraved below the surface, examples: steel die engraving, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravure" title="Gravure">gravure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etching</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collagraphy" title="Collagraphy">collagraph</a>.</li>
<li>Porous or <a href="/wiki/Stencil_printing" title="Stencil printing">Stencil</a>, in which the printing areas are on fine mesh screens through which ink can penetrate, and the non-printing areas are a stencil over the screen to block the flow of ink in those areas, examples: <a href="/wiki/Screen_printing" title="Screen printing">screen printing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stencil_duplicator" title="Stencil duplicator">stencil duplicator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Risograph" title="Risograph">risograph</a>.</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_marks">Crop marks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Crop marks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To print an image without a blank area around the image, the non-printing areas must be trimmed after printing.  Crop marks can be used to show the printer where the printing area ends, and the non-printing area begins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>  The part of the image which is trimmed off is called <a href="/wiki/Bleed_(printing)" title="Bleed (printing)">bleed</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letterpress">Letterpress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Letterpress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commercial._Le_Samedi_BAnQ_P48S1P03551.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="621" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Commercial._Le_Samedi_BAnQ_P48S1P03551.jpg/220px-Commercial._Le_Samedi_BAnQ_P48S1P03551.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Commercial._Le_Samedi_BAnQ_P48S1P03551.jpg/330px-Commercial._Le_Samedi_BAnQ_P48S1P03551.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Commercial._Le_Samedi_BAnQ_P48S1P03551.jpg/440px-Commercial._Le_Samedi_BAnQ_P48S1P03551.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Commercial._Le_Samedi_BAnQ_P48S1P03551.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miehle press printing Le Samedi journal. <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, 1939.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Letterpress_printing" title="Letterpress printing">Letterpress printing</a></div>
<p><b>Letterpress printing</b> is a technique of <a href="/wiki/Relief_printing" title="Relief printing">relief printing</a>. A worker composes and locks <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> into the bed of a press, <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">inks</a> it, and presses paper against it to transfer the ink from the type which creates an impression on the paper. 
There is different paper for different works the quality of paper shows different ink to use.
</p><p>Letterpress printing was the normal form of printing text from its invention by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Johannes Gutenberg</a> in the mid-15th century and <a href="/wiki/Global_spread_of_the_printing_press" title="Global spread of the printing press">remained in wide use for books and other uses</a> until the second half of the 20th century, when <a href="/wiki/Offset_printing" title="Offset printing">offset printing</a> was developed. More recently, letterpress printing has seen a revival in an artisanal form.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offset">Offset</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Offset">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Offset_press" title="Offset press">Offset press</a></div>
<p><b>Offset printing</b> is a widely used modern printing process. This technology is best described as when a positive (right-reading) image on a printing plate is inked and transferred (or "offset") from the plate to a rubber blanket. The blanket image becomes a mirror image of the plate image. An offset transfer moves the image to a printing substrate (typically paper), making the image right-reading again. Offset printing utilizes a <a href="/wiki/Lithography" title="Lithography">lithographic</a> process which is based on the repulsion of oil and water. The offset process employs a flat (planographic) image carrier (plate) which is mounted on a press cylinder. The image to be printed obtains <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">ink</a> from ink rollers, while the non-printing area attracts an (acidic) film of water, keeping the non-image areas ink-free. Most offset presses utilize three cylinders: Plate, blanket, impression. Currently, most books and newspapers are printed using offset lithography.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravure">Gravure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Gravure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rotogravure" title="Rotogravure">Rotogravure</a></div>
<p><b>Gravure printing</b> is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intaglio_printing" title="Intaglio printing">intaglio printing</a> technique, where the image being printed is made up of small depressions in the surface of the printing plate. The cells are filled with ink, and the excess is scraped off the surface with a doctor blade. Then a rubber-covered roller presses paper onto the surface of the plate and into contact with the ink in the cells. The printing cylinders are usually made from copper plated steel, which is subsequently chromed, and may be produced by diamond engraving; etching, or <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> ablation.
</p><p>Gravure printing is used for long, high-quality print runs such as magazines, mail-order catalogues, packaging and printing onto fabric and wallpaper. It is also used for printing postage stamps and decorative plastic laminates, such as kitchen worktops.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flexography">Flexography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Flexography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Flexography" title="Flexography">Flexography</a> is a type of relief printing. The relief plates are typically made from <a href="/wiki/Photopolymer" title="Photopolymer">photopolymers</a>.
The process is used for flexible packaging, corrugated board, labels, newspapers and more. In this market it competes with gravure printing by holding 80% of the market in USA, 50% in Europe but only 20% in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IzdebskaThomas2015_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-IzdebskaThomas2015-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_printing_techniques">Other printing techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other printing techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The other significant printing techniques include:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dye-sublimation_printer" title="Dye-sublimation printer">Dye-sublimation printer</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inkjet" title="Inkjet">Inkjet</a>, used typically to print a small number of books or packaging, and also to print a variety of materials: from high quality papers simulating offset printing, to floor tiles. Inkjet is also used to apply mailing addresses to direct mail pieces</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Laser_printing" title="Laser printing">Laser printing</a> (toner printing) mainly used in offices and for transactional printing (bills, bank documents). Laser printing is commonly used by direct mail companies to create variable data letters or coupons.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pad_printing" title="Pad printing">Pad printing</a>, popular for its ability to print on complex three-dimensional surfaces</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relief_print" title="Relief print">Relief print</a>, mainly used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mail-order_catalog" title="Mail-order catalog">catalogues</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Screen-printing" title="Screen-printing">Screen-printing</a> for a variety of applications ranging from T-shirts to floor tiles, and on uneven surfaces</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Intaglio_(printmaking)" title="Intaglio (printmaking)">Intaglio</a>, used mainly for high value documents such as currencies.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thermal_printing" title="Thermal printing">Thermal printing</a>, popular in the 1990s for fax printing. Used today for printing labels such as airline baggage tags and individual price labels in supermarket deli counters.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_German_movable_type_printing_press">Impact of German movable type printing press</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Impact of German movable type printing press">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantitative_aspects">Quantitative aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Quantitative aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:European_Output_of_Printed_Books_ca._1450%E2%80%931800.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="953" data-file-width="1121" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/European_Output_of_Printed_Books_ca._1450%E2%80%931800.png/220px-European_Output_of_Printed_Books_ca._1450%E2%80%931800.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/European_Output_of_Printed_Books_ca._1450%E2%80%931800.png/330px-European_Output_of_Printed_Books_ca._1450%E2%80%931800.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/European_Output_of_Printed_Books_ca._1450%E2%80%931800.png/440px-European_Output_of_Printed_Books_ca._1450%E2%80%931800.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:European_Output_of_Printed_Books_ca._1450%E2%80%931800.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>European output of books printed by movable type from ca. 1450 to 1800<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buringh_&amp;_van_Zanden_2009_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buringh_&amp;_van_Zanden_2009-32">[32]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_printing" title="History of printing">History of printing</a></div>
<p>It is estimated that following the innovation of Gutenberg's printing press, the European book output rose from a few million to around one billion copies within a span of less than four centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buringh_&amp;_van_Zanden_2009_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buringh_&amp;_van_Zanden_2009-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_impact">Religious impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Religious impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hartlib" title="Samuel Hartlib">Samuel Hartlib</a>, who was exiled in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> and enthusiastic about social and cultural reforms, wrote in 1641 that "the art of printing will so spread knowledge that the common people, knowing their own rights and liberties, will not be governed by way of oppression".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs-Burke_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs-Burke-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PrintMus_038.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2240" data-file-width="1778" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/PrintMus_038.jpg/220px-PrintMus_038.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/PrintMus_038.jpg/330px-PrintMus_038.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/PrintMus_038.jpg/440px-PrintMus_038.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PrintMus_038.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replica of the Gutenberg press at the <a href="/wiki/International_Printing_Museum" title="International Printing Museum">International Printing Museum</a> in Carson, California</div></div></div>
<p>In the Muslim world, printing, especially in Arabic scripts, was strongly opposed throughout the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>, partially due to the high artistic renown of the art of traditional calligraphy. However, printing in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_script" title="Armenian script">Armenian script</a> was often permitted.  Thus, the first movable type printing in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> was in Hebrew in 1493, after which both religious and non-religious texts were able to be printed in Hebrew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>  According to an imperial ambassador to <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> in the middle of the sixteenth century, it was a sin for the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a>, particularly Turkish Muslims, to print religious books. In 1515, Sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim I</a> issued a decree under which the practice of printing would be punishable by death. At the end of the sixteenth century, Sultan <a href="/wiki/Murad_III" title="Murad III">Murad III</a> permitted the sale of non-religious printed books in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> characters, yet the majority were imported from <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Muteferrika" title="Ibrahim Muteferrika">Ibrahim Muteferrika</a> established the first press for printing in Arabic in the Ottoman Empire, against opposition from the calligraphers and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">Ulama</a>. It operated until 1742, producing altogether seventeen works, all of which were concerned with non-religious, utilitarian matters.  Printing did not become common in the Islamic world until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hebrew language printers were banned from printing <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> in some Germanic states; as a result, Hebrew printing flourished in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, beginning in 1470 in Rome, then spreading to other cities including Bari, Pisa, Livorno, and Mantua. Local rulers had the authority to grant or revoke licenses to publish Hebrew books,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt2009_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt2009-36">[36]</a></sup> and many of those printed during this period carry the words 'con licenza de superiori' (indicating their printing having been officially licensed) on their title pages.
</p><p>It was thought that the introduction of printing 'would strengthen religion and enhance the power of monarchs.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyrowitz41_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyrowitz41-37">[37]</a></sup> The majority of books were of a religious nature, with the church and crown regulating the content. The consequences of printing 'wrong' material were extreme. Meyrowitz<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyrowitz41_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meyrowitz41-37">[37]</a></sup> used the example of <a href="/wiki/William_Carter_(martyr)" title="William Carter (martyr)">William Carter</a> who in 1584 printed a pro-Catholic pamphlet in Protestant-dominated England. The consequence of his action was <a href="/wiki/Hanging" title="Hanging">hanging</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_impact">Social impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Social impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Print gave a broader range of readers access to knowledge and enabled later generations to build directly on the intellectual achievements of earlier ones without the changes arising within verbal traditions. Print, according to <a href="/wiki/John_Dalberg-Acton,_1st_Baron_Acton" title="John Dalberg-Acton, 1st Baron Acton">Acton</a> in his 1895 lecture <i>On the Study of History</i>, gave "assurance that the work of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> would last, that what was written would be accessible to all, that such an occultation of knowledge and ideas as had depressed the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> would never recur, that not an idea would be lost".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs-Burke_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs-Burke-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Press1520.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5459" data-file-width="3764" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Press1520.png/170px-Press1520.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Press1520.png/255px-Press1520.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Press1520.png/340px-Press1520.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Press1520.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bookprinting in the 16th century</div></div></div>
<p>Print was instrumental in changing the social nature of reading.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Eisenstein" title="Elizabeth Eisenstein">Elizabeth Eisenstein</a> identifies two long-term effects of the invention of printing. She claims that print created a sustained and uniform reference for knowledge and allowed comparisons of incompatible views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Asa_Briggs" title="Asa Briggs">Asa Briggs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Burke_(historian)" title="Peter Burke (historian)">Peter Burke</a> identify five kinds of reading that developed in relation to the introduction of print:
</p>
<ol><li>Critical reading: Because texts finally became accessible to the general population, critical reading emerged as people were able to form their own opinions on texts.</li>
<li>Dangerous reading: Reading was seen as a dangerous pursuit because it was considered rebellious and unsociable, especially in the case of women, because reading could stir up dangerous emotions such as love, and if women could read, they could read love notes.</li>
<li>Creative reading: Printing allowed people to read texts and interpret them creatively, often in very different ways than the author intended.</li>
<li>Extensive reading: Once print made a wide range of texts available, earlier habits of intensive reading of texts from start to finish began to change, and people began reading selected excerpts, allowing much more extensive reading on a wider range of topics.</li>
<li>Private reading: Reading was linked to the rise of individualism because, before print, reading was often a group event in which one person would read to a group. With print, both literacy and the availability of texts increased, and solitary reading became the norm.</li></ol>
<p>The invention of printing also changed the occupational structure of European cities. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Printer_(publisher)" title="Printer (publisher)">Printers</a> emerged as a new group of artisans for whom literacy was essential, while the much more labour-intensive occupation of the <a href="/wiki/Scribe" title="Scribe">scribe</a> naturally declined. Proof-correcting arose as a new occupation, while a rise in the numbers of <a href="/wiki/Bookselling" title="Bookselling">booksellers</a> and librarians naturally followed the explosion in the numbers of books.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_impact">Educational impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Educational impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gutenberg's printing press had profound impacts on universities as well. Universities were influenced in their "language of scholarship, libraries, curriculum, [and] pedagogy"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_language_of_scholarship">The language of scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The language of scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Before the invention of the printing press, most written material was in Latin. However, after the invention of printing the number of books printed expanded as well as the vernacular. Latin was not replaced completely, but remained an international language until the eighteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="University_libraries">University libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: University libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At this time, universities began establishing accompanying libraries. "Cambridge made the chaplain responsible for the library in the fifteenth century but this position was abolished in 1570 and in 1577 Cambridge established the new office of university librarian. Although, the University of Leuven did not see a need for a university library based on the idea that professor were the library. Libraries also began receiving so many books from gifts and purchases that they began to run out of room. However, the issue was solved in 1589 by a man named Merton who decided books should be stored on horizontal shelves rather than <a href="/wiki/Lectern" title="Lectern">lecterns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Curriculum">Curriculum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Curriculum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The printed press changed university libraries in many ways. Professors were finally able to compare the opinions of different authors rather than being forced to look at only one or two specific authors. Textbooks themselves were also being printed in different levels of difficulty, rather than just one introductory text being made available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_of_printing_methods">Comparison of printing methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Comparison of printing methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_printing">Digital printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Digital printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>By 2005, <a href="/wiki/Digital_printing" title="Digital printing">digital printing</a> accounts for approximately 9% of the 45 trillion pages printed annually around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vj_ipg2-0_keynote_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-vj_ipg2-0_keynote-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Printing at home, an office, or an engineering environment is subdivided into:
</p>
<ul><li>small format (up to ledger size paper sheets), as used in business offices and libraries</li>
<li>wide format (up to 3' or 914mm wide rolls of paper), as used in drafting and design establishments.</li></ul>
<p>Some of the more common printing technologies are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Blueprint" title="Blueprint">blueprint</a> – and related chemical technologies</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daisy_wheel" title="Daisy wheel">daisy wheel</a> – where pre-formed characters are applied individually</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dot_matrix_printer" title="Dot matrix printer">dot-matrix</a> – which produces arbitrary patterns of dots with an array of printing studs</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Line_printer" title="Line printer">line printing</a> – where formed characters are applied to the paper by lines</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_printer" title="Thermal printer">heat transfer</a> – such as early fax machines or modern receipt printers that apply heat to special paper, which turns black to form the printed image</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inkjet" title="Inkjet">inkjet</a> – including bubble-jet, where ink is sprayed onto the paper to create the desired image</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrophotography" title="Electrophotography">electrophotography</a> – where <a href="/wiki/Toner" title="Toner">toner</a> is attracted to a charged image and then developed</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Laser_printing" title="Laser printing">laser</a> – a type of <a href="/wiki/Xerography" title="Xerography">xerography</a> where the charged image is written pixel by pixel using a laser</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Solid_ink" title="Solid ink">solid ink</a> printer – where <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a> sticks of ink are melted to make liquid ink or toner</li></ul>
<p>Vendors typically stress the total cost to operate the equipment, involving complex calculations that include all cost factors involved in the operation as well as the capital equipment costs, amortization, etc. For the most part, toner systems are more economical than inkjet in the long run, even though inkjets are less expensive in the initial purchase price.
</p><p>Professional <a href="/wiki/Digital_printing" title="Digital printing">digital printing</a> (using <a href="/wiki/Toner" title="Toner">toner</a>) primarily uses an electrical charge to transfer toner or liquid ink to the substrate onto which it is printed. Digital print quality has steadily improved from early color and black and white copiers to sophisticated colour digital presses such as the Xerox iGen3, the Kodak Nexpress, the HP <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigo_Digital_Press" title="Indigo Digital Press">Indigo Digital Press</a> series, and the InfoPrint 5000. The iGen3 and Nexpress use toner particles and the Indigo uses liquid ink. The InfoPrint 5000 is a full-color, continuous forms inkjet drop-on-demand printing system. All handle variable data, and rival offset in quality. Digital offset presses are also called direct imaging presses, although these presses can receive computer files and automatically turn them into print-ready plates, they cannot insert variable data.
</p><p>Small press and fanzines generally use <a href="/wiki/Digital_printing" title="Digital printing">digital printing</a>. Prior to the introduction of cheap photocopying, the use of machines such as the <a href="/wiki/Spirit_duplicator" title="Spirit duplicator">spirit duplicator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hectograph" title="Hectograph">hectograph</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mimeograph" title="Mimeograph">mimeograph</a> was common.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kiosk_self_service_payment.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Kiosk_self_service_payment.jpg/220px-Kiosk_self_service_payment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Kiosk_self_service_payment.jpg/330px-Kiosk_self_service_payment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Kiosk_self_service_payment.jpg/440px-Kiosk_self_service_payment.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kiosk_self_service_payment.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Printing payment self service kiosk</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="3D_printing">3D printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 3D printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/3D_printing" title="3D printing">3D printing</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/3D_printing" title="3D printing">3D printing</a> is a form of manufacturing technology where physical objects are created from <a href="/wiki/3D_modeling" title="3D modeling">three-dimensional digital models</a> using 3D printers. The objects are created by laying down or building up many thin layers of material in succession. The technique is also known as additive manufacturing, rapid prototyping, or fabricating.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gang_run_printing">Gang run printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Gang run printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gang_run_printing" title="Gang run printing">Gang run printing</a> is a method in which multiple printing projects are placed on a common paper sheet in an effort to reduce printing costs and paper waste. Gang runs are generally used with sheet-fed printing presses and <a class="external text" href="https://www.m3printing.com/blog/rgb-vs-cmyk.html" rel="nofollow">CMYK process color</a> jobs, which require four or eight separate plates that are hung on the plate cylinder of the press.  Printers use the term "gang run" or "gang" to describe the practice of placing many print projects on the same oversized sheet. Basically, instead of running one postcard that is 4 x 6 as an individual job the printer would place 15 different postcards on 20 x 18 sheet, therefore using the same amount of press time the printer will get 15 jobs done in roughly the same amount of time as one job.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Printed_electronics">Printed electronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Printed electronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Printed_electronics" title="Printed electronics">Printed electronics</a> is the manufacturing of electronic devices using standard printing processes. Printed electronics technology can be produced on cheap materials such as paper or flexible film, which makes it an extremely cost-effective method of production. Since early 2010, the printable electronics industry has been gaining momentum and several large companies, including <a href="/wiki/Bemis_Company" title="Bemis Company">Bemis Company</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Tool_Works" title="Illinois Tool Works">Illinois Tool Works</a> have made investments in printed electronics and industry associations including OE-A and FlexTech Alliance are contributing heavily to the advancement of the printed electronics industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Printing_terminologies">Printing terminologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Printing terminologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Printing <a href="/wiki/Terminology" title="Terminology">terminologies</a> are the specific terms used in the printing <a href="/wiki/Industry_(economics)" title="Industry (economics)">industry</a>.
</p>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1093669538">.mw-parser-output .portalbox{padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portalborder{border:solid #aaa 1px}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:175px;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portalborder>ul{padding:0.1em;background:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:first-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:last-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}</style><div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint plainlist portalbox portalborder tright" role="navigation">
<ul>

Gutenberg's form of metal movable type was extremely similar to the Korean Jikji's, which was printed 78 years prior to the Gutenberg Bible. <p><b>On the effects of Gutenberg's printing</b>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_printers_manuals">Early printers manuals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Printing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Early printers manuals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The classic manual of early hand-press technology is
</p>
<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMoxon1962">Moxon, Joseph (1962) [1683–1684].  Herbert, Davies; Carter, Harry (eds.). "Mechanick Exercises on the Whole Art of Printing" (reprint ed.). New York: Dover Publications.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=Mechanick+Exercises+on+the+Whole+Art+of+Printing&amp;rft.date=1962&amp;rft.aulast=Moxon&amp;rft.aufirst=Joseph&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APrinting"></span> <span class="cs1-hidden-error citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_journal" title="Template:Cite journal">cite journal</a>}}</code>: </span><span class="cs1-hidden-error citation-comment">Cite journal requires <code class="cs1-code">|journal=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#missing_periodical" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span></li></ul>
<dl><dd>A somewhat later one, showing 18th century developments is</dd></dl>
<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFStower1965">Stower, Caleb (1965) [1808]. "The Printer's Grammar" (reprint ed.).
